                    gptAdSlots["ad2"] = googletag.defineSlot('/116097782/jcde_Behind_Ad2',
                            [[300, 250], [160, 600], [300, 600]], 'adBlockMainBodyTop')
                        .defineSizeMapping(mapping_ad2)
                        .addService(googletag.pubads());
                    

                    
                    var mapping_ad3 = googletag.sizeMapping()
                        .addSize([768, 0], [[300, 250], [300, 600], [160, 600]])
                        .build();
                    gptAdSlots["ad3"] = googletag.defineSlot('/116097782/jcde_Behind_Ad3',
                        [[300, 250], [160, 600], [300, 600]], 'adBlockMainBodyBottom')
                        .defineSizeMapping(mapping_ad3)
                        .addService(googletag.pubads());
                    

                    
                var mapping_ad4 = googletag.sizeMapping()
                        .addSize([0,0], [320, 50])
                        .addSize([768, 0], [728, 90])
                        .build();
                    gptAdSlots["ad4"] = googletag.defineSlot('/116097782/jcde_Behind_Ad4',
                        [728, 90], 'adBlockFooter')
                        .defineSizeMapping(mapping_ad4)
                        .addService(googletag.pubads());
                    

                googletag.pubads().addEventListener('slotRenderEnded', function (event) {
                    if (!event.isEmpty) {
                        $('.js-' + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).each(function () {
                            if ($(this).find('iframe').length) {
                                $(this).removeClass('hide');
                            }
                        });
                    }
                });

                googletag.pubads().addEventListener('impressionViewable', function (event) {
                    if (!event.isEmpty) {
                        $('.js-' + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).each(function () {
                            var $adblockDiv = $(this).find('.js-adblock');
                            var $adText = $(this).find('.js-adblock-advertisement-text');
                            if ($adblockDiv && $adblockDiv.is(':visible') && $adblockDiv.find('*').length > 1) {
                                $adText.removeClass('hide');
                            }
                            else {
                                $adText.addClass('hide');
                            }
                        });
                    }
                });

                googletag.pubads().setTargeting("jnlspage", "article");
                googletag.pubads().setTargeting("jnlsurl", "jcde/article/8/6/1622/6448508");
                googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
                googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
            });
        </script>

        (function () {

    $(document).ready(function () {
        $('.article-top-widget').on('click', '.ati-toggle-trigger', function () {
            $(this).find('.icon-general-add, .icon-minus').toggleClass('icon-minus icon-general-add');
            $(this).siblings('.ati-toggle-content').toggleClass('hide');
        });

        // In Chrome, an anchor tag with target="_blank" and a "mailto:" href opens a new tab/window as well as the email client
        // I suspect this behavior will be corrected in the future
        // Remove the target="_blank"
        $('ul.wi-affiliationList').find('a[href^="mailto:"]').each(function () {
            $(this).removeAttr('target');
        });
    });
</script>

                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735831 id="318735831" class="abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title" >Abstract</h2>
<section class="abstract"><p class="chapter-para">Electrothermal microgrippers are nowadays commonly used as they are small in size, low cost, and easy to manufacture. The microgripper is modeled through partial differential equations, which are discretized by the finite element method (FEM), producing a large number of ordinary differential equations. This makes model order reduction (MOR) a fruitful proposition. Moreover, in design applications, the microgripper needs to be simulated repeatedly, with varying values of certain parameters. When these parameters are geometric in nature, matrix interpolation-based parametric model order reduction (pMOR) is the most suited method. However, in finite element applications, the sizes of the FE matrices change for varying geometrical parameters. The conventional matrix interpolation-based pMOR method becomes inapplicable for such cases, which remains a major drawback of this otherwise powerful algorithm. In this paper, this major hurdle is removed, and a new finite element compatible matrix interpolation (FEMI) for pMOR is proposed. A three-dimensional electrothermal microgripper is modeled parametrically using this proposed methodology. The effectiveness of the proposed method is shown through simulation results.</p></section>                    <div class="article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata"></div>
                    <div id="318735832"></div>

at-ArticleMetadata"></div>
<div class="kwd-group"><a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;micro-electro-mechanical systems (MEMS)&quot;">micro-electro-mechanical systems (MEMS)</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;electrothermal microgripper&quot;">electrothermal microgripper</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;finite element method (FEM)&quot;">finite element method (FEM)</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;model order reduction (MOR)&quot;">model order reduction (MOR)</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;parametric model order reduction (pMOR)&quot;">parametric model order reduction (pMOR)</a></div><div class="boxed-text boxed-matter no-caption"><span class="label">Highlights</span><div class="clearfix"></div><ul class="bullet"><li><p class="chapter-para">A three-dimensional microgripper is modeled.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">A new finite element compatible matrix interpolation for parametric model order reduction is proposed to cope with change of system dynamics for varying geometrical parameters.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">The proposed method is applied to the multiparameter approach through simulations.</p></li></ul></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735835 id="318735835" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec1>1. Introduction</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">In the last few decades, with the development of miniaturization techniques, the use of microgrippers has grown at a rapid pace from biological engineering to optical engineering, microrobotics, etc. A wide range of microgripper designs have been developed based on their actuation technologies such as electrostatic, electrothermal, pneumatic, piezoelectric, shape memory alloy actuation, etc. Among them, electrothermal microgrippers are widely used because of their low voltage operation, production of high output force, and handling and manipulating of micron or submicron objects with precision. The three most common forms of electrothermal microgripper designs are hot and cold arm, chevron, and bimorph designs. The hot-and-cold-arm actuator-based microgripper utilizes the asymmetrical thermal expansion of its components to operate (Solano &amp; Wood, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib31"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib31" data-open="bib31" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2007</a>; Somà <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib32"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib32" data-open="bib32" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2018</a>). The chevron actuator-based microgrippers employ one-directional thermal expansion (Chu <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib13"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib13" data-open="bib13" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2011</a>; Shivhare <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib30"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib30" data-open="bib30" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2016</a>). The bimorph actuator, on the other hand, depends on the difference in thermal expansion coefficients (CTE) of structural materials (Zheng <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib36"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib36" data-open="bib36" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2011</a>). The traditional U-shaped hot-and-cold-arm actuator with different widths of the arms was used in the microgripper in Chronis and Lee (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib12" data-open="bib12" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2005</a>). A polysilicon microgripper based on the different arm length principle is developed in Pan and Hsu (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib22" data-open="bib22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">1997</a>). A new microgripper with two hot arms is presented, and analysed in Dow <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib15"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib15" data-open="bib15" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2011</a>).</p><p class="chapter-para">Like most MEMS systems, electrothermal microgrippers are modeled through partial differential equations as distributed parameter systems. The finite element method (FEM) turns these partial differential equations into a large number of ordinary differential equations, making the whole process computationally expensive (Bathe &amp; Wilson, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib6"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib6" data-open="bib6" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">1976</a>; Civalek <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib14"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib14" data-open="bib14" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2020</a>; Akbaş <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib1" data-open="bib1" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2021</a>). Model order reduction (MOR) gives a lower dimensional approximation of the higher order original system (Schilders, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib28"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib28" data-open="bib28" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2008</a>). Further, in many applications, the system needs to be simulated repetitively for different values of a few parameters of the system. If these parameters can be preserved even in reduced models, then the full-order model (FOM) need not be synthesized, and reduced at each of these parameter values. This gives rise to the research area called parametric model order reduction (pMOR; Bruns &amp; Benner, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib10"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib10" data-open="bib10" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2015</a>).</p><p class="chapter-para">There are various pMOR methods proposed in the last few years. A comparative study of different pMOR methods can be found in Baur <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib9"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib9" data-open="bib9" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2017</a>). One strategy to determine the bases including a numerically stable framework is to use moment matching methods for parametrized systems, as presented in Baur <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib8" data-open="bib8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2011</a>). In the context of optimization, the technique is advantageous. Another contribution is the development, reduction, and evaluation of geometrically parametrized structural models as given in Ammar <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib2" data-open="bib2" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2014</a>). The authors used proper generalized decomposition to derive solutions by using geometric parameters as extra solution domain coordinates. Ionita and Antoulas (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib19" data-open="bib19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2014</a>) propose a new data-driven parametrized model reduction method based on multivariate rational interpolation for building reduced-order models that accurately simulate the frequency domain behavior of large-scale, parametrized, linear dynamical systems. In Poussot-Vassal and Roos (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib24"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib24" data-open="bib24" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2012</a>), a methodology is provided for deriving the linear parameter variable model from a set of large-scale Multiple Input Multiple Output Linear Time-Invariant (LTI) models describing a certain frozen system. The goal of Wittmuess <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib35"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib35" data-open="bib35" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2016</a>) is to provide a new way for extending the well-known balanced truncation method for MOR to parameter-dependent systems. For this procedure, discretization of the parameter space is not required. On the other hand, the system matrices’ derivatives concerning the parameters must be supplied.</p><p class="chapter-para">Many pMOR approaches rely on interpolation. In Amsallem and Farhat (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib3" data-open="bib3" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2008</a>), a reduced-order model adaption method based on interpolation in tangent space to a Grassmann manifold is provided. In Baur and Benner (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib7"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib7" data-open="bib7" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2009</a>), a pMOR method based on the coupling of balanced truncation with interpolation is presented. pMOR by matrix interpolation (MI) is introduced in Panzer <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib23" data-open="bib23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2010</a>) and Varona <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib34"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib34" data-open="bib34" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2017</a>), where the parameters are geometric in nature. Here, reduced-order models are constructed for different parameter values, and then these reduced models are interpolated to get a generalized reduced model. However, it is assumed here that the original system matrices are equidimensional. However, with the change in geometrical parameters, the finite element meshes and consequently the size of the models or the dimension of system matrices change invariably. Hence, the conventional MI-based pMOR method cannot be applied directly in such cases. Some rudimentary modifications to the conventional MI method have been proposed in Roy and Nabi (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib26" data-open="bib26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2018</a>). Here, in this article, the proposed method is enriched to a new finite element compatible matrix interpolation (FEMI) for pMOR and applied to the multiparameter approach of the electrothermal microgripper through simulations. The key features of this paper are summarized below.</p><ul class="roman-lower"><li><p class="chapter-para">This paper presents the modeling of electrothermal microgripper (Cauchi <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib11" data-open="bib11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2019</a>) with thorough validation.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">The proposed modeling methodology employs a new FEMI for pMOR.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">The given FEMI approach is established with an in-depth mathematical justification.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">The design parameters of the microgripper are geometric in nature and their influence has been studied.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">The proposed pMOR approach is implemented to the multiparameter design problem of electrothermal microgripper through simulation. The results with comparative analysis establish the effectiveness of the proposed FEMI for the pMOR method.</p></li></ul>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735841 id="318735841" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec2>2. Modeling of the Electrothermal Microgripper</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">A three-dimensional microgripper model, as defined in Cauchi <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib11" data-open="bib11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2019</a>), is considered here. A three-dimensional schematic of the microgripper is shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">1</a>. It is based on a hot-and-cold-arm actuation design, and consists of a pair of hot and cold arms, anchors, flexures, and tweezers as shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">2</a>. The entire microgripper is made of single-crystal silicon (SCS) with a pad metal layer consisting of a gold layer with a thin chromium adhesion layer on the anchor and the cold arm.</p>                    <a id="318735843" scrollto-destination="318735843"></a>

microgripper.</p></div></div>                    <a id="318735844" scrollto-destination="318735844"></a>

href="/view-large/figure/318735844/qwab066fig2.jpg" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig2.jpg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="318735844" href="/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/qwab066fig2.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&Signature=yuQ6fZMSx8zobEEW5GJvBgha-26EBZLwJFWe~B4k4Jnzj3VdbCHXkcmmQa-g-~vK0~LJbdnFo~Er8tJhEl9pA5aFoO5ft-0LfwvESg9LrUXpBxwQ6Hs8WyDJW1ucdbiJIm6nZvlNTz-QSIDykqkaO-XaCGCyeD9Pwk74xuZ2dwLgjimXmh3ppgpFtqI19CfKzvnhEXZP1CaJi4g4uX2SoJOux5apuxWmHIRPXy3MBiAK-aJcrPxmQJGo3~ViM6Z6E1KMwrAO71oFH4a-4XvgJz3HvrACKpoqF4VkMNa~rxESNxoHKRBtGsXaeGOWz2o6Sy-YBJ51lacWgS~BE~tZeA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=318735844&ar=6448508&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=6272" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig2.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Geometrical parameters of microgripper.</p></div></div><p class="chapter-para">In Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">2</a>, uniform voltage is impressed upon the pad metal in the anchors. As the width of the hot arm is smaller than the cold arm, it produces greater heating in the hot arm compared to the cold arm. Heat produces thermal stress and mechanical expansion in the polymer. Thus, the tweezers can move laterally toward the wider cold arm (Chronis &amp; Lee, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib12" data-open="bib12" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2005</a>). Modeling of this microgripper using FEM is described here.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=318735846 id="318735846" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec2-1>2.1. Electrothermal modeling</h3>
<div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">The temperature profile <em>T</em>(<em>x, y, z</em>) of the microgripper can be expressed with the heat transfer equation (Reddy, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib25"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib25" data-open="bib25" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">1993</a>) <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636421531679" content-id="update1636421531679"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
\rho C_p\frac{\partial T}{\partial t}-\triangledown (\kappa \triangledown T) + \beta P (T-T_{\infty })= q(t) ,
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(1)</span></div>where ρ is the mass density of the material, <em>C<sub>p</sub></em> is the specific heat constant of the material, κ is the thermal conductivity, β is the convection heat transfer coefficient, and depends on the structural dimensions and the operating temperature, and <em>T</em><sub>∞</sub> is the temperature of the surrounding medium. The quantity <em>q</em>(<em>t</em>) is given as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636421611355" content-id="update1636421611355"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
q(t)=\frac{V^2(t)}{R} .
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(2)</span></div>Here, <em>V</em>(<em>t</em>) is the input voltage injected uniformly to the anchor’s pad metal layer and <em>R</em> is the electrical resistance of pad metal. Now applying FEM, (1) becomes <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636421676959" content-id="update1636421676959"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
{{\bf \bar{C}}}\dot{\bar{T}}(t)+({{\bf K}}+{{\bf H}}){\bar{T}(t)}={{\bf P}}+{{\bf Q}}q(t) ,
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(3)</span></div>where <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}(t)}$|</span> is a large vector consisting of temperatures at nodes, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf \bar{C}}}$|</span> is the capacitance matrix, <strong>K</strong> is the stiffness matrix, <em>q</em>(<em>t</em>) is the input, and <em>q</em>(<em>t</em>)’s distribution throughout the device is described by a vector <strong>Q</strong>. The additional coefficient matrices <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf H}}\,\text{and}\, {{\bf P}}$|</span> are due to the convection boundary conditions.</div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=318735848 id="318735848" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec2-2>2.2. Thermo-mechanical modeling</h3>
<div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">The method for detailed thermo-mechanical modeling can be found in Hughes (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib18"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib18" data-open="bib18" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2012</a>) and Bathe (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib5" data-open="bib5" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">1996</a>). The change in temperature produces thermal stress <em>f</em>, which can be calculated using <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636421750007" content-id="update1636421750007"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
f(t)= {{\bf C}}{\begin{bmatrix}\alpha _1\\
\alpha _2\\
\alpha _3\\
0\\
0\\
0\\
\end{bmatrix}}({\bar{T}}_i-{\bar{T}}(t)) ,
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(4)</span></div>where α is thermal expansion coefficient, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}}_i$|</span> is the initial temperature, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}}$|</span> is the produced temperature. The matrix <strong>C</strong> consists of elastic stiffness coefficients of material, and <em>C<sub>i, j</sub></em> can be expressed in terms of material constants as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="" content-id=""><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
C_{11} &amp;=&amp; \frac{1-\nu _{yz} \nu _{zy}}{E_{y}E_{z}\Delta } \nonumber \\
C_{22} &amp;=&amp; \frac{1-\nu _{xz} \nu _{zx}}{E_{x}E_{z}\Delta } \nonumber \\
C_{33} &amp;=&amp; \frac{1-\nu _{xy} \nu _{yx}}{E_{x}E_{y}\Delta }
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div></div><div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="" content-id=""><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
C_{12} &amp;=&amp; \frac{\nu _{yx}+\nu _{zx}\nu _{yz}}{E_{y}E_{z}\Delta } = \frac{\nu _{xy}+\nu _{zy}\nu _{xz}}{E_{x}E_{z}\Delta } \nonumber \\
C_{13} &amp;=&amp; \frac{\nu _{zx}+\nu _{yx}\nu _{zy}}{E_{y}E_{z}\Delta } = \frac{\nu _{xyz}+\nu _{xy}\nu _{yz}}{E_{x}E_{z}\Delta }\nonumber \\
C_{23} &amp;=&amp; \frac{\nu _{zy}+\nu _{xy}\nu _{zx}}{E_{x}E_{z}\Delta } = \frac{\nu _{yz}+\nu _{yx}\nu _{xz}}{E_{x}E_{z}\Delta }\nonumber \\
C_{44} &amp;=&amp; G_{yz}, C_{55} = G_{zx}, C_{66} = G_{xy}\\
\Delta &amp;=&amp; \frac{1-\nu _{xy}\nu _{yx}-\nu _{yz}\nu _{zy}-\nu _{xz}\nu _{zx}-2\nu _{yx}\nu _{zy}\nu _{xz}}{E_{x}E_{y}E_{z}}.
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">The thermo-mechanical relation is governed by three governing equations. The strain–displacement relation and the stress–strain relation can be written as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="equ5" content-id="equ5"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
\varepsilon ={{\bf D}}u \quad \mathrm{and}\quad \sigma = {{\bf C}}\varepsilon
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(5)</span></div>where <em>u</em>, ε, and σ denote the displacement vector, strain vector, and the stress vector, respectively, with <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="" content-id=""><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
D^{T}={\begin{bmatrix}\displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial x} &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial z} &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial y}\\
0 &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial y} &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial z} &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial x}\\
0 &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial z} &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial x} &amp; \quad \displaystyle\frac{\partial }{\partial y} &amp; \quad 0 \end{bmatrix}}.
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div></div>The principle of virtual displacements for the three-dimensional elasticity problem can be written in vector form as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636421917929" content-id="update1636421917929"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
&amp;&amp; \sum _{m}\int _{V^{(m)}}{\bf D}^{(m)T}{\bf C}^{(m)}{\bf D}^{(m)}U \mathrm{ d} V^{(m)} \nonumber \\
&amp;&amp; \quad -\,\sum _{m}\int _{V^{(m)}}{\bf H}^{(m)T}\left[ f^{(m)}-\rho ^{(m)}{\bf H}^{(m)}\ddot{U}-b^{(m)}{\bf H}^{(m)}\dot{U}\right]\mathrm{ d} V^{(m)}=0 .
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(6)</span></div>The finite element approximation is assumed to be of the form <em>u</em> = <strong>H</strong><em>U</em>, where <strong>H</strong> is the displacement interpolation matrix, <em>m</em> denotes element <em>m, b</em> is the damping property parameter of element <em>m</em>, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$U =\lbrace u^1_x,u^1_y,u^1_z,\dots ,u^n_x,u^n_y,u^n_z\rbrace$|⁠</span>. Substituting this into equation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-update1636421917929"></span><a href="#update1636421917929" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">6</a>), we finally arrive at the finite element model as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636421982249" content-id="update1636421982249"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
{{\bf M}}\ddot{U}(t)+{{\bf \bar{D}}}\dot{U}(t)+{{\bf \bar{K}}}U(t)=F(t),
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(7)</span></div>where stiffness matrix <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf \bar{K}}}=\sum _{m}\int _{V^{(m)}}{\bf D}^{(m)T}{\bf C}^{(m)}{\bf D}^{(m)}\mathrm{ d}V^{(m)}$|⁠</span>, mass matrix <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf M}}=\sum _{m}\int _{V^{(m)}}\rho ^{(m)}{\bf H}^{(m)T}{\bf H}^{(m)}\mathrm{ d} V^{(m)}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf \bar{D}}}=\sum _{m}\int _{V^{(m)}}b^{(m)}{\bf H}^{(m)T}{\bf H}^{(m)}\mathrm{ d} V^{(m)}$|⁠</span>, load vector <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$F=\sum _{m}\int _{V^{(m)}}{\bf H}^{(m)T} f^{(m)}\mathrm{ d} V^{(m)}$|⁠</span>, ρ<sup>(<em>m</em>)</sup> is the mass density of element <em>m</em>, and <em>b</em><sup>(<em>m</em>)</sup> is the damping property parameter of element <em>m</em>.</div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735851 id="318735851" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec3>3. pMOR of the Electrothermal Microgripper</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">Various design strategies of the electrothermal actuator in the microgripper have been proposed such as two or three beam structures (Hashiguchi &amp; Fujita, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib17"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib17" data-open="bib17" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2002</a>; Kolesar <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib20"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib20" data-open="bib20" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2002</a>). In low-voltage applications, the performance of the microgripper is influenced by geometrical variations, as shown in Shamshirsaz <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib29"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib29" data-open="bib29" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2008</a>) and Pahwa <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib21"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib21" data-open="bib21" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2012</a>); i.e. longer actuator arms help in larger displacements. Similarly, as shown in Cauchi <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib11" data-open="bib11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2019</a>), with the change in cold arm width, the displacement changes. In this paper, for modeling the microgripper, referring to Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">2</a>, the actuator arm length and the width of the cold arm are two main geometric design parameters taken here as <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$p_1 \text{ and } p_2$|⁠</span>, respectively. Further, the effect of actuator arm length and the cold arm width on displacement can be seen in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">3</a>, justifying the choice of these two parameters.</p>                    <a id="318735853" scrollto-destination="318735853"></a>
<div data-id="fig3" class="fig fig-section js-fig-section"><div class="label fig-label">Figure 3:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" displacement of microgripper.</p></div></div><div content-id="fig3" class="fig fig-modal reveal-modal"><div class="label fig-label">Figure 3:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" src="https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/m_qwab066fig3.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&amp;Signature=KL-B8NN3n~UsP3Kda6FsgJo584dcs-GkkWiX5xkW642hkp1NGQX6D1Ztj0znEvulsULMB~AuwlT3d3B~HivXLdjrHgisCF5yGs14hVoGEdncUxFw6xrZnVAd-Ho-lpL~0tKt2PBzxjxdrkNqTP0MjSQWfWrzawAigyFZVAMx0g66k-zr8xZUgo4sDbO1kesWAO3nD8AyynqV2fwf1KFpsakX6dB1QcKYkoXTY7KNa8~Dfpx87eRyYyAd0KvclrPj5lDl7tXmUZmKW1-CddGBNoTVjtpiWntY-wNZlvLx7u4KP68wGbCKBz0880JuQXAEaC2Rt6dpdguug9qHlPTI6A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA" alt="Influence of geometrical parameters on the displacement of microgripper." data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig3.jpg" /><div class="fig-orig original-slide"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow" href="/view-large/figure/318735853/qwab066fig3.jpg" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig3.jpg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="318735853" href="/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/qwab066fig3.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&Signature=fKZLxsZw0Z~1lYHa9i7ED~dqL67Kx4NjrgeGI-UH07GgbpHmj3CXMwbTWveaz4iKtBxLUy0X3nVrKA91rXdGzA8HsD3DeuNl1JfGorfRohkPc3oSd33ds35m-c6jFHNvxOKy-yjkK6FzUj7u-58SG5Jd3l0KQWRy5a7jJLRsVmUSBRa-8ZoCymXAfm1WKuOZhVcUycHcRxhhkTtbkEtpQQGg81cYC6YY5pZSKtrct6oXuE6Sj-z8zClOmHkVAwRpgzhaGb5hrTcU0uHnSM2MJztw-~jCSsNt~RObH~Q1ujrSw3fErJXFbLWiYSPgjOGMDL7hZsu3YtMJTN6K74gagw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=318735853&ar=6448508&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=6272" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig3.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Influence of geometrical parameters on the displacement of microgripper.</p></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">Considering these two parameters <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$p_1\text{ and } p_2$|⁠</span>, equations (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-update1636421676959"></span><a href="#update1636421676959" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">3</a>) and (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-update1636421982249"></span><a href="#update1636421982249" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">7</a>) become <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="equ8" content-id="equ8"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
&amp;&amp; {{\bf \bar{C}}}(p_1,p_2)\dot{{\bar{T}}}(t)+({{\bf K}}(p_1,p_2)+{{\bf H}}(p_1,p_2)){\bar{T}}(t) \nonumber \\
&amp;&amp; \quad = {{\bf P}}(p_1,p_2)+{{\bf Q}}(p_1,p_2)q(t)
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(8)</span></div><div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427352491" content-id="update1636427352491"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
{{\bf M}}(p_1,p_2)\ddot{U}(t)+{{\bf D}}(p_1,p_2)\dot{U}(t)+{{\bf \bar{K}}}(p_1,p_2)U(t)=F(t) ,
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(9)</span></div>where <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf \bar{C}}}(p_1,p_2) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_1 \times N_1}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf K} \in \mathbb {R}^{N_1 \times N_1}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf Q}} \in \mathbb {R}^{N_1 \times 1}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}}(t) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_1 \times 1}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf M}}(p_1,p_2) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_2 \times N_2}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf D}}(p_1,p_2) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_2 \times N_2}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf \bar{K}}}(p_1,p_2) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_2 \times N_2}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$F(t) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_2 \times 1}$|⁠</span>, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$U(t) \in \mathbb {R}^{N_2 \times 1}$|⁠</span>. Here, <em>N</em><sub>1</sub> and <em>N</em><sub>2</sub> are quite large. Considering the modeling of this large-scale system depending on such parameters, the conventional MOR techniques become computationally expensive. It may be recalled that retaining the parameter dependence is the main purpose of the pMOR, thus allowing the variation of parameters without the need of generating the original model, and repeating the reduction step. Hence, new reduced models can be obtained directly for any parameter value, shortening the design process drastically.</div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=318735855 id="318735855" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>3.1. Electrothermal system: first-order pMOR</h3>
<div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">Consider the large dynamic state-space system described in equation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-equ8"></span><a href="#equ8" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">8</a>). The order of the system is indicated here as <em>n</em> and assumed to be very large. This system is projected to a lower order subspace spanned by the columns of orthogonal transformation matrix <strong>V</strong>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) obtained through first-order Arnoldi algorithm as <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$\bar{T}(t)={{\bf V}}(p_1,p_2) \bar{T}_r(t)$|⁠</span>, where <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf V}}(p_1,p_2) \in \mathbb {R}^{n \times r}$|</span> and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}_r} \in \mathbb {R}^{r \times 1}$|⁠</span>. With this, the reduced model finally can be written as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427541924" content-id="update1636427541924"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
&amp;&amp; {{\bf C}}_r(p_1,p_2)\dot{\bar{T}}_r(t)+({{\bf K}}_r(p_1,p_2)+{{\bf H}}_r(p_1,p_2))\bar{T}_r(t) \nonumber \\
&amp;&amp; \quad ={{\bf P}}_r(p_1,p_2)+{{\bf \bar{Q}}}_r(p_1,p_2)q(t) ,
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(10)</span></div>where <strong>C</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) = <strong>V</strong><sup><em>T</em></sup>(·, ·)<strong>C</strong>(·, ·)<strong>V</strong>(·, ·), <strong>K</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) = <strong>V</strong><sup><em>T</em></sup>(·, ·)<strong>K</strong>(·, ·)<strong>V</strong>(·, ·), <strong>H</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) = <strong>V</strong><sup><em>T</em></sup>(·, ·)<strong>H</strong>(·, ·)<strong>V</strong>(·, ·), <strong>P</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) = <strong>V</strong><sup><em>T</em></sup>(·, ·)<strong>P</strong>(·, ·), and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf \bar{Q}}}_r(p_1,p_2)={{\bf V}}^T(\cdot ,\cdot ){{\bf \bar{Q}}}(\cdot ,\cdot )$|⁠</span>, with <em>r</em> being the order of the reduced model and <em>r</em> &lt; &lt;<em>n</em> (Salimbahrami &amp; Lohmann, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib27"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib27" data-open="bib27" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2002</a>).</div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=318735857 id="318735857" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>3.2. Thermo-mechanical system: second-order pMOR</h3>
<div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">Similarly, Krylov subspace-based reduction method applied to the second-order system reduces large dimensional system, preserving the second-order structure of the system as described in Bai and Su (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib4" data-open="bib4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2005</a>). The second-order Arnoldi reduction algorithm produces orthogonal projection matrix <strong>V</strong><sub><em>d</em></sub>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) with <em>U</em>(<em>t</em>) = <strong>V</strong><sub><em>d</em></sub>(<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>)<em>U<sub>r</sub></em>(<em>t</em>). Considering second-order equation in equation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-update1636427352491"></span><a href="#update1636427352491" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">9</a>), the reduced model can be written as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427689444" content-id="update1636427689444"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
{\bf M}_r(p_1,p_2)\ddot{U}_r(t)+{\bf D_r}(p_1,p_2)\dot{U}_r(t)+{\bf \bar{K}_r}(p_1,p_2)U_r(t)=F_r(t),
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(11)</span></div>where <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf M}_r(p_1,p_2)={{\bf V}}_d^T(\cdot ,\cdot ){{\bf M}}(\cdot ,\cdot ) {{\bf V}}_d(\cdot ,\cdot )$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf D}_r(p_1,p_2)={{\bf V}}_d^T(\cdot ,\cdot ){{\bf D}}(\cdot ,\cdot ){{\bf V}}_d(\cdot ,\cdot )$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf \bar{K}}_r(p_1,p_2)={{\bf V}}_d^T(\cdot ,\cdot ){{\bf \bar{K}}}(\cdot ,\cdot ) {{\bf V}}_d(\cdot ,\cdot )$|⁠</span>, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$F_r(p_1,p_2)={\bf {{\bf V}}_d^T(\cdot ,\cdot )}F(\cdot ,\cdot )$|⁠</span>, with <em>r</em> being the order of the reduced model.</div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735859 id="318735859" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec4>4. Review of MI-Based pMOR</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">The pMOR should be in a position to handle any number of parameters and structures in which the parameter dependence in the matrices is not analytically expressed. One of such pMOR methods is MI-based pMOR, introduced in Panzer <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib23" data-open="bib23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2010</a>). The method is described pictorially in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">4</a>. With the knowledge of FOMs and reduced-order models at points <em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>,...<em>p<sub>k</sub></em>, generalized reduced-order model can be obtained at any point like <em>p<sub>ij</sub></em>. For verification, generated MI-based reduced model can be compared with the original FOM and directly reduced model at <em>p<sub>ij</sub></em>.</p>                    <a id="318735861" scrollto-destination="318735861"></a>
<div data-id="fig4" class="fig fig-section js-fig-section"><div class="label fig-label">Figure 4:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig4.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Pictorial representation of pMOR with MI.</p></div></div><div content-id="fig4" class="fig fig-modal reveal-modal"><div class="label fig-label">Figure 4:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" src="https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/m_qwab066fig4.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&amp;Signature=ziuYblc8LmotCOnHXpsW5Q6nnb03B0syeZboPDnkvswm6IYi3louT-3AiXZY8Gts4~1E0-8FWm7xCpB4mt-1E~COtQwqCDuQIL5LhFdobRbEDWKI-R-iXpbZILl1e3D1bnbFzZdDeus39F1SutxhG-fZsx~N2~-KXnAl3lNoBY82eHcLU29JkKBTShrtDiOCYWs70-4leGpJDG-izlXhZR931upJpwFtw9qD091~I3gv3ejbA6MirndMqr2ZKM4j6FIoZK5VkOV5YF40xhf4vo70M2eCHipwcYjTPtomjCn5RrrgVSc2Yah9UEWCO9uQW0U7uBHGs31W153JnlOMRw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA" alt="Pictorial representation of pMOR with MI." data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig4.jpg" /><div class="fig-orig original-slide"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow" href="/view-large/figure/318735861/qwab066fig4.jpg" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig4.jpg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="318735861" href="/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/qwab066fig4.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&Signature=XP6y9xdsjYTEnk-T68VqYzBtVMvtDCZdOAlzNetnbbusvypss2mvYlVfl7XxpCNUx30Lo0mS~cutp0890ICfkn2RgKgEHAoyr2wcVJvxODncq-7OXEv6UrM4cAgmY6xWnK~0tHtUUhdAnPcF~i~ARkG214ysC9tQWtdG2r3w8lvfDspWwnbyBuYo-xqik2aGpkDLqt20cbSaX-6JxfX8JFC13IqYAizUZgmm2nsuP6NlwzQlaOq44dc1foUkjc-5NJcHKX8SeR7Pe2vk-IbB~VJjB8oQzJfSKOEBMuXnTjVD5WaCl3blkEyCbVfz8mfQb841f4f4d4ODpVhd0GFO9g__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=318735861&ar=6448508&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=6272" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig4.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Pictorial representation of pMOR with MI.</p></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">Consider the general equations of a large-order LTI state-space system <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427816162" content-id="update1636427816162"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{\bf E}(p)\dot{x}(t) &amp;=&amp; {\bf A}(p)x(t) + {\bf B}(p)u(t) \nonumber \\
{y}(t) &amp;=&amp; {\bf C}(p)x(t),
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(12)</span></div>where the state-space matrices <strong>E</strong>(<em>p</em>), <strong>A</strong>(<em>p</em>), <strong>B</strong>(<em>p</em>), and <strong>C</strong>(<em>p</em>) are dependent on a parameter vector <em>p</em>.</div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">This system is projected to a lower dimensional subspace with an orthogonalized matrix <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf V} \in \mathbb {R}^{n \times r}$|⁠</span>, as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="equ13" content-id="equ13"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
{x}(t) \approx {\bf V(p)} x_r(t)
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(13)</span></div>to get a reduced-order state-space system of order <em>r</em> as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427850462" content-id="update1636427850462"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{{\bf E}}_{r}(p) \dot{x}_{r}(t) &amp; = &amp; {{\bf A}}_{r}(p) x_{r}(t) + {{\bf B}}_{r}(p) u(t) \nonumber \\
{y}(t) &amp; = &amp; {\bf C}_{r}(p) {x}_{r}(t),
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(14)</span></div>where <strong>E</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em>) = <strong>W<sup>T</sup></strong>(<em>p</em>)<strong>E</strong>(<em>p</em>)<strong>V</strong>(<em>p</em>), <strong>A</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em>) = <strong>W<sup>T</sup></strong>(<em>p</em>)<strong>A</strong>(<em>p</em>)<strong>V</strong>(<em>p</em>), <strong>B</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em>) = <strong>W<sup>T</sup></strong>(<em>p</em>)<strong>B</strong>(<em>p</em>), and <strong>C</strong><sub><em>r</em></sub>(<em>p</em>) = <strong>C</strong>(<em>p</em>)<strong>V</strong>(<em>p</em>), where <strong>W<sup>T</sup></strong>(<em>p</em>) is another orthogonal matrix. The number of inputs <em>u</em> and the number of outputs <em>y</em> remain the same.</div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">The continuous parametric space, <em>p</em>, is uniformly sampled and a discrete set of parameters <em>p<sub>i</sub></em> is obtained for <em>i</em> = 1, 2,...<em>k</em>. At each discrete parameter value, large-scale models and reduced models are generated with individual projection matrices <strong>W</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>, <strong>V</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>, given by <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427905237" content-id="update1636427905237"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{\bf E}_{r,i} \,\, {\dot{x}}_{r,i}(t) &amp; = &amp; {\bf A}_{r,i} \,\, { x}_{r,i}(t) + {\bf B}_{r,i} \,\, {u}(t) \nonumber \\
{ y}(t) &amp; = &amp; {\bf C}_{r,i} \,\, {x}_{r,i}(t).
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(15)</span></div></div><p class="chapter-para">At different discrete points in the parametric space, reduced-order models have been obtained with different projection matrices <strong>W</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>, <strong>V</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>. As the reduced-order states may not be compatible with each other, direct interpolation may become erroneous.</p><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">Hence, all the local reduced models (<strong>E</strong><sub><em>r, i</em></sub>, <strong>A</strong><sub><em>r, i</em></sub>, <strong>B</strong><sub><em>r, i</em></sub>, <strong>C</strong><sub><em>r, i</em></sub>) need to be adjusted before interpolation. With state transformation <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf T}_i^{-1}$|</span> and a premultiplying matrix <strong>M</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>, these models can be interpolated correctly as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636427958563" content-id="update1636427958563"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{{\bf E}}^{*}_{r,i} { \dot{x}}^{*}_{r,i}(t) &amp; = &amp; {{\bf A}^{*}}_{r,i}{x}^{*}_{r,i}(t) +{{\bf B}}^{*}_{r,i} { u}(t) \nonumber \\
{y}(t) &amp; = &amp; {{\bf C}}^{*}_{r,i} {x}^{*}_{r,i}(t),
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(16)</span></div>with <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf E}}^{*}_{r,i}={{\bf M}_i {\bf E}_{r,i} {\bf T}_i^{-1}}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf A}}^{*}_{r,i}={{\bf M}_i {\bf A}_{r,i} {\bf T}_i^{-1}}$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf B}}^{*}_{r,i}={{\bf M}_i {\bf B}_{r,i}}$|⁠</span>, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf C}}^{*}_{r,i}={{\bf E}_{r,i} {\bf T}_i^{-1}}$|⁠</span>. In Panzer <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib23" data-open="bib23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2010</a>), it has been shown that it is possible to get the consistent state vectors <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$x^{*}_{r,i}$|</span> with a matrix <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf R} \in \mathbb {R}^{n \times r}$|</span> whose columns span the universal subspace, by choosing <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${{\bf T}}^{-1}_i$|</span> appropriately. Specifically, the following option has been shown for the purpose. <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="equ17" content-id="equ17"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{\bf T}_i &amp; = &amp; {\bf R}^T {\bf V}_i
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(17)</span></div><div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="equ18" content-id="equ18"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{\bf M}_i &amp; = &amp;({\bf W}_i^T {\bf R} )^{-1}
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(18)</span></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">While applying one-sided Arnoldi algorithm, <strong>W</strong> = <strong>V</strong>. Now, the following has been proposed for obtaining <strong>R</strong>, whose <em>r</em> columns span the universal subspace of the MOR approach. The projection matrices <strong>V</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>s form an independent pool of directions and are therefore combined as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636428077644" content-id="update1636428077644"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
{\bf V}_{\mathrm{ all}} = [ {\bf V}_1 \,\, {\bf V}_2\,\, \cdots {\bf V}_k ] .
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(19)</span></div>This is followed by its singular value decomposition (SVD), given by <strong>V</strong><sub>all</sub> = <strong>U Σ N</strong><sup><em>T</em></sup>. The first <em>r</em> columns of <strong>U</strong> span <em>r</em> different directions and serve as <strong>R</strong>.</div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735868 id="318735868" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec5>5. Mathematical Basis for FE compatible MI for pMOR</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">The abovementioned basic MI method proves to be good for the design method involving geometric parameters. However, it has a certain shortcoming. It was assumed that irrespective of the parameter values, the original large system was of the same size, denoted here as <em>n</em>. However, in most of the systems simulated or designed by the FEM, a slight change in the geometry will require a new mesh. Consequently, whenever parameter value is changed, the size of the system matrices obtained through the FEM changes due to change in the generated mesh. Similarly, for the current microgripper problem, the size of the FE system matrices changes as the length of the hot and cold arms or the breadth of the cold arm changes.</p><p class="chapter-para">Now, projection matrices project the large order dynamics of this large system to a lower order subspace. As the dimension of the large original systems varies, the size of the projection matrices should also change. However, in the conventional MI method, for the <em>i</em>-th set of parameter values, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf V}_i(p_i) \in \mathbb {R}^{n \times r}$|</span> is a matrix of constant size, irrespective of <em>i</em>. In the proposed generic methodology, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf V}_i(p_i) \in \mathbb {R}^{n_i \times r}$|⁠</span>, where <em>n<sub>i</sub></em> is the order of the large system corresponding to the <em>i</em>-th set of parameter values and <em>r</em> is the order of the reduced system, kept same for all the parameter values. Further, for brevity, the parameter <em>p</em> is dropped henceforth, denoting <strong>V</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>(<em>p<sub>i</sub></em>) simply as <strong>V</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>.</p><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">This set of <strong>V</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub>, being of different row size, cannot be accumulated in a matrix <strong>V</strong><sub>all</sub>, like in equation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-update1636428077644"></span><a href="#update1636428077644" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">19</a>). It may be recalled that this matrix <strong>V</strong><sub>all</sub>, constituted a “pool” from which <em>r</em> directions would be ultimately chosen in the matrix <strong>R</strong>. To obtain <strong>V</strong><sub>all</sub> in the present case, the following adjustment needs to be done, which can be illustrated by a simple example. As shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">5</a>, a vector <em>v</em><sub>1</sub> in <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$\mathbb {R}^{1}$|</span> space could be embedded in <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$\mathbb {R}^{2}$|</span> space as <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$v_1^{\prime }= [ v_1 \,\, 0]$|⁠</span>, and further as <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$v_1^{\prime \prime }= [ v_1 \,\, 0 \,\, 0 ]$|</span> in <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$\mathbb {R}^{3}$|⁠</span>. This embedding in a larger space does not disturb the direction of the vector <em>v</em><sub>1</sub>. In other words, we get the vector <em>v</em><sub>1</sub> back unmodified if we add <strong>0</strong> to any vector <em>v</em><sub>1</sub>. Because the zero vector is a vector of length zero. The zero vector does not point in any particular direction; hence, it has an undefined direction (Szabo, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib33"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib33" data-open="bib33" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2015</a>). Similarly, assuming <em>k</em> models are involved in the interpolation, the block matrices <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf V}_i \in \mathbb {R}^{n_i \times r}$|</span> can be collected in a matrix <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636428464171" content-id="update1636428464171"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
\begin{array}{r c l}\bf V^{\prime }_{\mathrm{ all}} &amp;=&amp; {\begin{bmatrix}\bf \hat{V}_{1} &amp; \quad {\bf \hat{V}}_{2} &amp; \quad \cdots &amp; \quad {\bf \hat{V}}_{k} \end{bmatrix}} \\
&amp;=&amp; \begin{bmatrix}\bf V_{11} &amp; \quad {\bf V}_{21} &amp; \quad \cdots &amp; \quad {\bf V}_{k1} \\
0 &amp; \quad {\bf V}_{22} &amp; \quad \cdots &amp; \quad {\bf V}_{k2} \\
0 &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \cdots &amp; \quad {\bf V}_{k3} \\
0 &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \ddots &amp; \quad \vdots \\
0 &amp; \quad 0 &amp; \quad \cdots &amp; \quad {\bf V}_{k k} \end{bmatrix} \in \mathbb {R}^{n_k \times kr} ,\end{array}
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(20)</span></div>where the column blocks represent <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf V}_{1}={\bf V}_{11} \in \mathbb {R}^{n_1 \times r} \,\, ; \,\, {\bf V}_{2}=[{\bf V}^T_{21} \,\, {\bf V}^T_{22}]^T \in \mathbb {R}^{n_2 \times r}$|</span> and so on. The total number of rows is <em>n<sub>k</sub></em>, the number of nodes in the largest mesh, corresponding to the <em>k</em>-th set of parameter values.</div><ol class="number"><li><p class="chapter-para">As adding zero vector does not include any other information, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf \hat{V}}_{1}, {\bf \hat{V}}_{2}, \cdots ,{\bf \hat{V}}_{k}$|</span> remain orthogonal, i.e. <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf \hat{V}}^{T}_{i}{\bf \hat{V}}_{i}=I$|⁠</span>.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">Rank and nullity of <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf \hat{V}}_{i}$|</span>’s do not change.</p></li></ol>                    <a id="318735873" scrollto-destination="318735873"></a>
<div data-id="fig5" class="fig fig-section js-fig-section"><div class="label fig-label">Figure 5:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" alt="A vector embedded in higher subspaces." data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig5.jpg" /><div class="fig-orig original-slide"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow" href="/view-large/figure/318735873/qwab066fig5.jpg" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig5.jpg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="318735873" href="/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/qwab066fig5.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&Signature=ug73pBomYj5UZaNnfbyUsYXiS4rEVycNg6m3KxGzrfRjhs9enLZpULQtPVo6RMrMqIiHU01ebKYQUujKVQllctM29f067ntu-MqRILP~NlCIceAnmtHqOoBNsIw4P2nk9eGxgzu~9V58JMqUooQtavjBqaybyw887V3EqZexHSjRS7DoMSV2tyO4Mo8ym3ML0sbBk-kxZDlCZUPT7dZ6wxP1OBErWrcY3fEJWI-oiSO~G0hS6H0MlLI9f6rRgdf9ALmGu~xWXzEL3dhEiQ5Ql4NEPsQd69FkWEwMp9Dnu-ylu2xBPLf8Pg77uvumsnEAnlCiLnmUaMPJQr~2ZOBAzw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=318735873&ar=6448508&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=6272" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig5.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">A vector embedded in higher subspaces.</p></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">Next, as discussed before, the SVD of <strong>V<sup>′</sup></strong><sub>all</sub> is calculated with <strong>V<sup>′</sup></strong><sub>all</sub> = <strong>U Σ N</strong><sup><em>T</em></sup>. As in the conventional MI method, the first <em>r</em> columns of <strong>U</strong> are chosen as <strong>R</strong>, guaranteeing the capture of the most important directions. Hence, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf R} \in \mathbb {R}^{n_k \times r}$|⁠</span>, and using <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="" content-id=""><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
\begin{array}{ccccc}\bf T_i &amp; = &amp; {\bf R}^T {\bf {\hat{V}}}_i &amp; \in &amp; \mathbb {R}^{r \times r} \\
{\bf M}_i &amp; = &amp;({\bf {\hat{V}}}_i^T {\bf R} )^{-1} &amp; \in &amp; \mathbb {R}^{r \times r} \end{array}
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div></div><strong>T</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub> and <strong>M</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub> are computed. The matrix <strong>R</strong> is orthogonal, the reduced local models contain state vectors compatible with R, and they are all stated as the basis of R. <strong>T</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub> and <strong>M</strong><sub><em>i</em></sub> are nonsingular. In the above, instead of <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf V}_{i} \in \mathbb {R}^{n_i \times r}$|⁠</span>, the augmented vector <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf \hat{V}}_{i} \in \mathbb {R}^{n_k \times r}$|</span> is to be used for consistency of multiplication.</div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">To get a global reduced model, a weighted interpolation is done on the set of transformed reduced models, as given in equation (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-update1636427905237"></span><a href="#update1636427905237" class="sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec">15</a>). The distance between the intermediate point or interpolant (desired parameter value) and the predefined discretization points determines the weight distribution for interpolation. A general way of finding weight function is <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="equ21" content-id="equ21"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
w_i=\frac{\hat{w}_i}{\sum _{i=1}^{k}\hat{w}_i}
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(21)</span></div>where <em>w<sub>i</sub></em> are the weights assigned to the discretization points, with <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\hat{w}_i}=(1/d_i^{\beta })$|⁠</span>, where<em>d<sub>i</sub></em> is the distance between the interpolant and discretization points and β is a positive constant and user defined. The most viable choice is β = 2. For the two-parameter problem, the distance can be calculated as Euclidean distance. If the intermediate point is positioned as described in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig6"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">6</a>, then the model will be influenced by the nearest discretization points and they will have higher weights. As the distance increases, the models will have a lesser influence on the interpolant, and weights will be lesser depending on the distance.</div>                    <a id="318735876" scrollto-destination="318735876"></a>

content-id="fig6" class="fig fig-modal reveal-modal"><div class="label fig-label">Figure 6:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" href="/view-large/figure/318735876/qwab066fig6.jpg" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig6.jpg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="318735876" href="/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/qwab066fig6.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&Signature=iSWkLHKe2d1z0nyTsbEBU7bi8MMztymkMfJHphsMfbUQsQuzHnL8Xm~61otwAEP4lo409-Yh6HiN5WeH30BPAX8uLPzYyfoZv7GvxT3YAeS9wuJTp~PtBGdTOTZSpnpN4WB2ijhBAgfeWAJF0iHBvoFoggh7vbnoVeetejv8Otm7P6nQLoQFENN2N0-k22RFZQxAg8pIfMOW0BlHcshOaurJVVFzDaw-4WzAHw83TSQrla5g-YZ-uR~eou5w3mvg7zrNgfJzC~pLWVpxnNjOso615p7tlwpua8SCfb0Zp7hjfSKiRp0a3-JRll7ER5zU7slDrk6yw8JVfVEpbRiCeg__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=318735876&ar=6448508&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=6272" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig6.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Parametric space.</p></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">The global reduced model can be found as <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636428832193" content-id="update1636428832193"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{eqnarray*}
{\bf E}_{r} \,\, {\dot{x}}^{*}_{r}(t) &amp; = &amp; {\bf A}_{r} \,\, { x}^{*}_{r}(t) + {\bf B}_{r} \,\, {u}(t) \nonumber \\
{ y}(t) &amp; = &amp; {\bf C}_{r} \,\, {x}^{*}_{r}(t),
\end{eqnarray*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(22)</span></div>where <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf E}_{r}=\sum _{i=1}^{k}w_iE^{*}_i$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf A}_{r}=\sum _{i=1}^{k}w_iA^{*}_i$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf B}_{r}=\sum _{i=1}^{k}w_iB^{*}_i$|⁠</span>, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bf C}_{r}=\sum _{i=1}^{k}w_iC^{*}_i$|</span> with <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|$\sum _{i=1}^{k}w_i=1$|⁠</span>. A pseudo-code demonstrating the application of FEMI for pMOR to the microgripper problem in order to generate the reduced-order model for any parameter value is given as follows:</div>                    <a id="318735878" scrollto-destination="318735878"></a>

problem.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735879 id="318735879" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec6>6. Results and Simulations</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">The considered electrothermal microgripper is made of SCS and a layer of pad metal consisting of gold and chromium as shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">1</a>. SCS layer has a thickness of 25 <em>μ</em>m, and pad metal layer consists of 0.5 <em>μ</em>m thickness of the gold and 20 nm thickness of chromium. Standard values of the thermal and mechanical constants have been taken and are given in Table <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-tbl1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="tbl1" data-open="tbl1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">1</a>. It is modeled in the Gmsh software (Geuzaine &amp; Remacle, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib16"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib16" data-open="bib16" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2009</a>) and simulated using MATLAB<sup>®</sup>.</p>                    <a id="318735881" scrollto-destination="318735881"></a>

        </a></div></div></div><div class="table-full-width-wrap"><div class="table-wrap table-wide"><div class="table-wrap-title" id="tbl1" data-id="tbl1"><span class="label">Table 1:</span><div class="caption"><p class="chapter-para">Thermal and mechanical parameters (Cauchi <em>et al</em>., <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib11" data-open="bib11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2019</a>).</p></div> </div><div class="table-overflow"><table><thead><tr><th>Parameters<span aria-hidden="true" style="display: none;">

          Open in new tab
        </a></div></div></div><p class="chapter-para">It is assumed that heat is not radiated to the environment from the microgripper. The system’s initial temperature is considered as 300 K, the ambient temperature. Throughout the simulation, the system’s anchors are kept at 300 K temperature. Input voltage of 12 V is impressed uniformly upon the pad metal of the anchor. The generated heat creates thermal stress that produces displacement. Here, in modeling, the damping constant is considered to be 0.25. The gold layer is considered to have no displacement.</p><p class="chapter-para">In this parametric problem, two parameters have been taken, which are the actuator arm length, denoted by <em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, and the width of the cold arm, denoted by <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>. The microgripper is initially modeled with <em>p</em><sub>1</sub> = 200 <em>μ</em>m and <em>p</em><sub>2</sub> = 55 <em>μ</em>m to reproduce the result of Cauchi <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib11" data-open="bib11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2019</a>) for initial validation. The displacement for different input voltage is shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig7"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig7" data-open="fig7" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">7</a> and it agrees with Cauchi <em>et al</em>. (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-bib11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="bib11" data-open="bib11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr">2019</a>). Further, as shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig8" data-open="fig8" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">8</a>, <em>p</em><sub>1</sub> is varied from 195 to 203 <em>μ</em>m and <em>p</em><sub>2</sub> is varied from 54 to 57 <em>μ</em>m with equidistant interpolation points defined in between. At the discretization points, full-order electrothermal models and thermo-mechanical models are generated. The number of ODEs generated through FE modeling is quite high; for example, at (<em>p</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>p</em><sub>2</sub>) = (200, 55), the order of the electrothermal model is 3997 and the thermo-mechanical model is 9660. Hence, the FE models are projected to a lower dimensional subspace of dimension taken uniformly as <em>r</em><sub>1</sub> = 15. In Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig9"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig9" data-open="fig9" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">9</a>, the singular values of the electrothermal model and thermo-mechanical model are shown. Considering most dominant singular values, the order of the reduced models can be considered 15. Though there is no global method available for choosing the order of the reduced models. With different parameter values, the sizes of the unreduced original systems and locally reduce models are given in Table <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-tbl2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="tbl2" data-open="tbl2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">2</a>.</p>                    <a id="318735884" scrollto-destination="318735884"></a>

          Open in new tab
        </a></div></div></div><p class="chapter-para">To check the efficacy of the proposed method, a set of arbitrary intermediate parametric points are chosen as <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_1:(p_1,p_2) = (196, 54.5)$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_2:(p_1,p_2) = (198, 56.7)$|⁠</span>, <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_3:(p_1,p_2) = (200, 55.3)$|⁠</span>, and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_4:(p_1,p_2) = (202.4, 56.3)$|</span> as shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig8" data-open="fig8" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">8</a>. Using the 20 reduced models obtained at the discretization points, the proposed FE compatible MI-based reduced models are obtained at any intermediate parametric point. These models obtained through FE compatible MI are projected back to the original large space and their results are compared with those obtained from the unreduced FOMs and directly reduced models for verification.</p><p class="chapter-para">As an example, in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig8" data-open="fig8" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">8</a> the intermediate parametric point <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_1$|</span> is taken to generate a model with improved MI. Using this model, the evolution of temperature at the hottest zone in the hot arm of the microgripper is shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig10"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig10" data-open="fig10" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">10</a>. Within 0.2 seconds, the temperature profile reaches its steady-state condition. The displacement at the microgripper tip is shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig11" data-open="fig11" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">11</a>. Here too, the displacement reaches its steady state within 0.2 seconds. The steady-state temperature and displacement of the original model, directly reduced model, and the model obtained through FEMI are shown in Figs <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig12" data-open="fig12" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">12</a>, <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig13"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig13" data-open="fig13" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">13</a>, and <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig14"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig14" data-open="fig14" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">14</a>, respectively. The plots clearly show the perfect agreement between the interpolated model and the unreduced original model, as well as the directly reduced model. In Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig15"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig15" data-open="fig15" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">15</a>, the Bode plots for electrothermal model and thermo-mechanical model for the intermediate parametric point <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_1$|</span> are shown. Similarly, in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig16"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig16" data-open="fig16" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">16</a>, the Bode plots for electrothermal model and thermo-mechanical model for the intermediate parametric point <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_4$|</span> are shown. The responses of the FOMs, directly reduced models, and reduced models obtained through FEMI-based pMOR agree with each other over the frequency range.</p>                    <a id="318735890" scrollto-destination="318735890"></a>

16:</div><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" src="https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/m_qwab066fig16.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&amp;Signature=k-60~eyFxie5QNAdrCWplJLTV6ZbQMysJzTtCH6pwxTZMuvNgN90aaKmoG1UqVXELGjmo5LbjB6zJeLpCOnqZB0tNVPV5qYsstIN~cnMR~L8B0QKDUDo5Cyn6C2BAAA0RzEvgkxKBYk-oVRfH4WdcKmPCq7UPoa8uuCIzCsgtPGONKSh19ReGIPgP~A5CxIU-LLdh~f4DCrLk0D5jfErqhVZPdYF-tmphUH1PcIrCsfsYG25WBcdO8-JCSmMub-GxadQZWze9a4NwxwtWhqA9OGv7FTt2b4lyOioDYKQ9JcLXICus02Q1yKhhBhTR~LqCokUnnlp3486k1cWpnYT3g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA" alt="Bode plots at parametric point ${\bar{p}}_4$ obtained through improved MI." data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig16.jpg" /><div class="fig-orig original-slide"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow" href="/view-large/figure/318735896/qwab066fig16.jpg" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig16.jpg" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a data-section="318735896" href="/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/jcde/8/6/10.1093_jcde_qwab066/1/qwab066fig16.jpeg?Expires=1643556756&Signature=rILajC6RkozQwbtk1YdmzwD2CSUR4HSiMAryNMBb4wCLx9FLeOYbs-zxBlxxSueBkoFqulso2684MesDdBpxNWhgKjBdZ9DpAsY6cW9tFJgf7AZLFY2LlQn~eD3DScwVuUAeTFstIFO07c3Jpdk1mnO8V4ASQ6a0MkgD09BgZfcBz0SR0tansvxDrqrinLm59uU0WUYm9RPN1tWa2EzgY5BII5HIFnkYijVrg9JWrnRIqFU-PC6HEcIWNXrxePRkQ8qw4L1Sw24zdOaDsUH0j7nruql49PaM7QYLSkCYviL-bGtucnqD-YKYSfW~TQZi4cdSx7VT8sCSD4k~-HSlqw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=318735896&ar=6448508&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=6272" class="download-slide" data-path-from-xml="qwab066fig16.jpg">Download slide</a></div></div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Bode plots at parametric point <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{p}}_4$|</span> obtained through improved MI.</p></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">For comparative analysis, relative errors of the temperature evolution, <em>e</em><sub>thermal</sub> at the hottest point of the microgripper, can be calculated using <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636432845457" content-id="update1636432845457"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
e_{\mathrm{ thermal}}=\left(\frac{{\bar{T}}_{\mathrm{ FOM}} -{\bar{T}}_{\mathrm{ FEMI}}}{{\bar{T}}_{\mathrm{ FOM}}} \right)\cdot 100\% ,
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(23)</span></div></div><div class="&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            ">where <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}}_{\mathrm{ FOM}}$|</span> is the temperature profile obtained from the unreduced FOM and <span class="inline-formula no-formula-id">|${\bar{T}}_{\mathrm{ FEMI}}$|</span> is the temperature profile obtained from the FEMI-based models. The generated relative errors for the electrothermal models at the intermediate parametric points are shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig17"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig17" data-open="fig17" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">17</a>. It can be seen that the relative errors at those points converge in the range of 0.01–0.0754%. Similarly, the relative errors of displacements <em>e</em><sub>displacement</sub> at the tip of the microgripper can be calculated utilizing <div class="formula-wrap"><div class="disp-formula" id="update1636433065731" content-id="update1636433065731"><div class="tex-math display-math">$$\begin{equation*}
e_{\mathrm{ displacement}}=\left(\frac{{U}_{\mathrm{ FOM}} -{U}_{\mathrm{ FEMI}}}{U_{\mathrm{ FOM}}} \right)\cdot 100\% ,
\end{equation*}
$$</div></div><span class="label">(24)</span></div>where <em>U</em><sub>FOM</sub> is the displacement at the tip obtained from the unreduced FOM of the microgripper and <em>U</em><sub>FEMI</sub> is the displacement at the tip obtained from FEMI-based models. The generated relative errors for the thermo-mechanical models at intermediate parametric points are shown in Fig. <span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-fig18"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="fig18" data-open="fig18" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">18</a>.</div>                    <a id="318735899" scrollto-destination="318735899"></a>

All the simulations are done in an ordinary laptop with Intel(R) Core(TM) i5-6300U CPU@ 2.40GHz 2.50 GHz processor, 16GB RAM.</p>                    <a id="318735905" scrollto-destination="318735905"></a>

          Open in new tab
        </a></div></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735906 id="318735906" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title" data-legacy-id=sec7>7. Conclusion</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">In this paper, the issue of modeling a three-dimensional electrothermal microgripper is addressed. Since the number of ODEs generated through FE modeling of the microgripper is quite high, MOR is used to produce lower order approximations of the system. Moreover, in design applications, for varying values of the design parameters, the simulations need to be carried out repeatedly. When these parameters are geometric, MI-based pMOR is used.</p><p class="chapter-para">In the microgripper model considered in this paper, the length of the hot arm and the width of the cold arm are important and have been taken as the parameters of interest. For every change in values of the parameters, new FE models are generated expectedly with different sizes of mesh and different sizes of system matrices. The conventional MI-based pMOR method is not capable of dealing with such scenarios. Hence, to cope with this, a new algorithm called FEMI for pMOR is proposed here. A set of arbitrary intermediate points is selected to verify the effectiveness of the proposed method. At these points, comparisons of simulation results for the models generated by the FEMI for pMOR with the models obtained by direct reduction, as well as the original unreduced models, prove the efficacy of the proposed method. The method promises to reduce finite element simulation time, which accelerates the design process. The approximation quality of the parametric reduced model depends significantly on the choice of the interpolating functions, where it is highly problem dependent to choose the appropriate interpolation functions. The approximation quality is also dependent on parameter discretization.</p><p class="chapter-para">In future research, other parameters will be investigated and incorporated into the design process using pMOR approaches. The influence of the sampling will be explored. This approach will also be investigated for nonlinear parameter dependence to make the FEMI for pMOR more robust. The port-Hamilton system can be used to model the electrothermal microgripper.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735910 id="318735910" class="backsection-title js-splitscreen-backsection-title" data-legacy-id=sec8>Conflict of interest statement</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">None declared.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=318735912 id="318735912" class="backreferences-title js-splitscreen-backreferences-title" data-legacy-id=ref1>References</h2>
